



You said:
im going to use gemma3:12b via docker. I have already downloaded gemma3:12b via ollama desktop app. how can I verify

ChatGPT said:

Thought for 19s
You can verify in two layers:

“Do I actually have gemma3:12b downloaded locally (via Ollama Desktop)?”

“Can my Docker setup see/use that same downloaded model?”

1) Verify it’s downloaded in Ollama (on your host)
Run these on the machine where Ollama Desktop is installed:

ollama list
You should see something like gemma3:12b in the list. (This is the simplest confirmation.) 

For more detail:

ollama show gemma3:12b
And a functional test:

ollama run gemma3:12b
If it starts an interactive prompt without pulling anything, it’s already present locally.

You can also verify via the local HTTP API:

curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags
That returns all locally available models (and includes metadata like a digest). 

(Optional) confirm the daemon is up:

curl http://localhost:11434/api/version

2) Verify Docker can use the same model (two common setups)
Option A (recommended): Docker talks to the host Ollama Desktop (no re-download)
This means your container does not run its own Ollama server; it just calls the host’s Ollama API.

macOS / Windows (Docker Desktop):

curl http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags
If you see gemma3:12b in that output, your container can “see” the same models Ollama Desktop has. 

Linux:
Use host networking (simplest) and then the same curl:

docker run --rm --network=host curlimages/curl:latest \
  curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags
If gemma3:12b shows up, you’re good.

If your app/container needs an env var, Ollama’s host/port is typically http://host.docker.internal:11434 (mac/win) or http://localhost:11434 with --network=host (linux).

Option B: Run Ollama inside Docker (models are stored in Docker’s volume)
If you run the official Ollama container, it will keep models in its own Docker volume by default:

docker run -d -p 11434:11434 -v ollama:/root/.ollama --name ollama ollama/ollama

Then verify from your host:

curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags
If gemma3:12b is not listed, that’s expected: this container has its own model store, separate from Ollama Desktop, unless you explicitly mount the same model directory.

Quick “yes/no” checklist
ollama list on host shows gemma3:12b ✅

curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags shows gemma3:12b ✅ 

From inside Docker, curl http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags (mac/win) shows it ✅

or on Linux: container with --network=host can curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags ✅

If you tell me your OS (macOS/Windows/Linux) and whether you’re running Ollama in Docker or just an app container that calls Ollama Desktop, I can give the exact one-liner verification command for your setup.

ChatGPT said:
That error is expected because you ran it on Windows (the host).

host.docker.internal is a special DNS name that normally only resolves inside containers (Docker Desktop provides it for containers to reach the host). On the host itself, Windows DNS won’t know it → Could not resolve host.

Here’s how to verify everything correctly on Windows 11 + Docker Desktop.

1) Verify Ollama is running and serving on the host (Windows)
In PowerShell:

curl http://localhost:11434/api/version
Then list models via the API:

curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags
And via the CLI:

ollama list
If gemma3:12b shows up in ollama list and/or /api/tags, it’s downloaded and available on your host.

2) Verify a Docker container can reach your host’s Ollama
Run curl inside a container:

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/version
Then check tags:

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags
If those return JSON, Docker can reach your host Ollama.

If that container test still fails
Sometimes host.docker.internal isn’t set up in a given network / config. Force it:

docker run --rm --add-host=host.docker.internal:host-gateway curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/version
(That --add-host ... host-gateway trick often fixes name resolution issues.)

3) Quick “did it actually use the already-downloaded model?” test
From the host:

ollama run gemma3:12b "say hello in one short sentence"
If it runs immediately without “pulling”, it’s using the local copy.

Common gotchas (Windows)
Docker Desktop must be in Linux containers mode (typical default). If you’re using Windows containers, networking behaves differently.

If curl http://localhost:11434/api/version fails on the host, Ollama isn’t running (or port is blocked).

If host curl works but container curl fails, it’s usually DNS mapping (--add-host=...) or Windows firewall.

If you paste the output of:

curl http://localhost:11434/api/version

and the output/error from the container command in step 2
…I can tell you exactly which case you’re in.

ChatGPT said:
Nice — you’ve already verified the important parts:

ollama list shows gemma3:12b ✅ (downloaded)

ollama run gemma3:12b responds ✅ (works)

Host API works on localhost:11434 ✅

A container can reach the host via host.docker.internal:11434 ✅ (your curlimages/curl test worked)

So your model is downloaded on the host, and Docker containers can use it.

Why curl http://host.docker.internal:11434/... failed on Windows host
Because host.docker.internal is meant for containers → host, not host → host. On Windows itself, DNS won’t resolve it. Your container test proves it’s fine.

What went wrong with your Docker images
1) nvidia/cuda:12.0-base “not found”
That tag doesn’t exist as written (or was renamed). Use a tag that actually exists, e.g.:

docker run --rm --gpus all nvidia/cuda:12.0.0-base-ubuntu22.04 nvidia-smi
(You can also use nvidia/cuda:12.4.1-base-ubuntu22.04, etc.)

If GPU is set up correctly in Docker Desktop, that should print your RTX 3060 info.

2) ai/gemma3:12b “not found”
That’s not a Docker image name on Docker Hub. gemma3:12b is an Ollama model name, not a container image.

To run Gemma3 in Docker you have two valid approaches:

Approach A (best): Keep Ollama on Windows, run your app in Docker
Your container just calls Ollama:

Test from container (you already basically did):

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/generate -d "{\"model\":\"gemma3:12b\",\"prompt\":\"Reply with ACTIVE\"}"
If it returns a JSON response containing “ACTIVE”, Docker can generate using your already-downloaded model.

For your actual app, set the base URL to:

http://host.docker.internal:11434 (from inside containers)

Approach B: Run Ollama inside Docker (separate model store unless you mount it)
This runs an Ollama server in a container:

docker run -d --name ollama -p 11434:11434 -v ollama:/root/.ollama ollama/ollama
But note: it will not automatically use the models you downloaded with Ollama Desktop unless you bind-mount the same model directory (and on Windows that path mapping can be annoying). So Approach A is usually simpler.

Quick verification checklist (you’re basically done)
Host has model: ollama list ✅

Host API sees it: curl http://localhost:11434/api/tags ✅

Container can see host Ollama: container curl to host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags ✅

Container can generate with it: use /api/generate test above ✅ (run it and you’re 100%)

One more thing: GPU acceleration
Ollama on Windows will use the GPU if supported by your setup/model build. You can verify utilization by:

Running ollama run gemma3:12b and watching Task Manager → Performance → GPU (CUDA/3D usage),

Or nvidia-smi in another terminal (works best if you have the NVIDIA tools installed).

If you want, paste the output of:

ollama show gemma3:12b
and I’ll tell you what it indicates about GPU layers / runtime behavior.

ChatGPT said:
You’re very close. Two things are now confirmed:

GPU passthrough in Docker works (your nvidia-smi inside nvidia/cuda succeeded) ✅

Your container can reach Ollama on the host (/api/version and /api/tags worked from the container) ✅

The only failure is the /api/generate call — that’s just PowerShell quoting/escaping. The JSON body you passed ended up with extra backslashes, so Ollama couldn’t parse it.

Here are copy/paste-safe ways to do it on Windows PowerShell.

Option 1 (easiest): use curl with single quotes inside the container
PowerShell won’t treat single quotes specially inside the container command:

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/generate `
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" `
  -d '{"model":"gemma3:12b","prompt":"Reply with ACTIVE","stream":false}'
You should get back JSON containing something like "response":"ACTIVE" (or “ACTIVE.”).

Option 2: use PowerShell to build JSON correctly, then pass it in
This avoids escaping pain entirely:

$body = @{ model="gemma3:12b"; prompt="Reply with ACTIVE"; stream=$false } | ConvertTo-Json -Compress
docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/generate `
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" `
  -d "$body"
Option 3: verify with /api/chat instead (also useful)
docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/chat `
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" `
  -d '{"model":"gemma3:12b","messages":[{"role":"user","content":"Reply with ACTIVE"}],"stream":false}'
Why your command failed
You used:

-d "{\"model\":\"gemma3:12b\",\"prompt\":\"Reply with ACTIVE\"}"
PowerShell + the backticks + quoting caused the JSON to arrive with literal escape sequences (extra \\), so Ollama saw malformed JSON and returned:

invalid character '\\' looking for beginning of object key string

Final “yes it works” check
Once one of the commands above returns a valid response, you’ve proven:

Docker container ↔ host Ollama connectivity ✅

Docker container can actually invoke gemma3:12b via the host Ollama ✅

Your already-downloaded model is being used (because the host is serving it) ✅

If you paste the output of Option 1, I’ll tell you exactly what field to look at (and how to disable streaming / get just the text).

Model is downloaded on Windows (Ollama Desktop store)

A Docker container can reach host Ollama

container curl http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/version works ✅

container curl http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags lists gemma3:12b ✅

A Docker container can actually generate using your host-downloaded model
Your final response:

"response":"ACTIVE\n"
✅ That confirms the container is invoking gemma3:12b via the host Ollama.

Why the earlier /api/generate failed
PowerShell escaping added extra backslashes (you got invalid character '\\'...). Using single quotes around JSON fixed it.

GPU verification (quick reality check)
You already verified Docker GPU passthrough with nvidia-smi ✅.

To verify Ollama is using the GPU during inference:

Run ollama run gemma3:12b and generate a longer response

While it’s generating, run (in another terminal):

nvidia-smi -l 1
If you see VRAM/Utilization jump and a process appears, Ollama is using the GPU.

Note: your earlier nvidia-smi output showed no running processes, which is normal because nothing was actively using CUDA at that exact moment.

One-liner you can reuse for verification anytime
docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/generate `
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" `
  -d '{"model":"gemma3:12b","prompt":"Reply ACTIVE","stream":false}'
If it returns "response":"ACTIVE...", you’re good.

Got it. Let’s make this boring, deterministic, and verifiable—starting with Docker only. Below is a step-by-step “Docker health audit” for Windows 11 that produces clear pass/fail outputs you can paste back. We’ll avoid Openclaw details for now.

Phase 0 — Record baseline (no changes)
Run these in PowerShell:

docker version
docker info
docker context ls
docker ps
Pass criteria

docker version shows both Client and Server (server = Docker Desktop engine).

docker info shows OSType: linux (you want Linux containers).

docker ps runs without error.

If any of these fail, stop here—Docker itself isn’t stable yet.

Phase 1 — Confirm WSL2 + backend (critical on Win11)
wsl --status
wsl -l -v
Pass criteria

Default version is 2

Your installed distros (often docker-desktop and docker-desktop-data) are Version 2

Phase 2 — Confirm DNS + outbound network from containers
This catches “gateway was a mess” issues early.

docker run --rm busybox nslookup docker.io
docker run --rm busybox wget -qO- https://example.com | head
Pass criteria

nslookup docker.io returns IPs

wget prints HTML (any output)

If DNS fails here, everything upstream will be flaky (pulls, service discovery, host access).

Phase 3 — Confirm host ↔ container routing
3A) Container ➜ Host (host.docker.internal)
You already proved this works, but we’ll re-check cleanly:

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/version
Pass criteria

returns {"version":"..."}

3B) Host ➜ Container (published ports)
Start a tiny web server:

docker run -d --name webtest -p 127.0.0.1:8089:80 nginx:alpine
curl http://127.0.0.1:8089
docker rm -f webtest
Pass criteria

curl returns nginx HTML

This confirms Windows port binding + NAT are sane.

Phase 4 — Confirm GPU in Docker (you did this, but lock it in)
Run:

docker run --rm --gpus all nvidia/cuda:12.0.0-base-ubuntu22.04 nvidia-smi
Pass criteria

Shows RTX 3060 and driver info (as you saw)

Phase 5 — Confirm bridge network behavior + isolation
We verify Docker networking isn’t “special” or broken.

docker network ls
docker network inspect bridge | Select-String -Pattern "Subnet|Gateway"
Then run a container and show its IP + gateway:

docker run --rm busybox sh -lc "ip a; echo '---'; ip r; echo '---'; cat /etc/resolv.conf"
Pass criteria

Route shows a default gateway like default via 172.x.x.1 dev eth0

/etc/resolv.conf contains a nameserver (often 192.168.65.5 or similar in Docker Desktop)

If gateway/DNS looks weird here, it explains “gateway was a mess.”

Phase 6 — Confirm compose + reproducibility
docker compose version
Pass criteria

Prints a version, no errors.

What I need from you (only Docker outputs)
Paste the outputs (or errors) from Phase 0–3 and Phase 5. Those are the highest-signal for systemic networking issues. (GPU is already ✅.)

Once Docker is clean, we’ll move to Openclaw with the same approach:

service graph + ports

explicit healthchecks

deterministic “smoke tests” for LLM, gateway, and reasoning path

logs + metrics checkpoints at every hop

One thing to note (not a failure, just important):
docker info shows HTTP Proxy / HTTPS Proxy set to http.docker.internal:3128. That can matter later if the proxy goes down or blocks some domains. Keep it in mind as a potential “flakiness” source.

Fix Phase 2 properly (PowerShell-safe)
Use commands that work in PowerShell:

2A) Outbound HTTPS test (show first lines without head)

Phase 3 — Host ↔ container routing (next checkpoint)
You already passed container ➜ host for Ollama, but we’ll do the generic port test too.

3A) Container ➜ Host (generic)
docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/version

3B) Host ➜ Container (published port)
docker run -d --name webtest -p 127.0.0.1:8089:80 nginx:alpine

docker rm -f webtest
Pass: you get the nginx welcome HTML.

Phase 5 — Network + gateway sanity (high value for your “gateway was a mess” history)
Run these exactly:

docker network ls
docker network inspect bridge | Select-String -Pattern "Subnet|Gateway"
docker run --rm busybox sh -lc "ip a; echo '---'; ip r; echo '---'; cat /etc/resolv.conf"
Pass criteria

ip r shows a default route via the docker bridge gateway

/etc/resolv.conf has a nameserver (often 192.168.65.x on Docker Desktop)

One small PowerShell tip (so we don’t hit this again)
Linux pipes like | head don’t work in PowerShell.

Use Select-Object -First N in PowerShell, or run sh -lc "... | sed -n '1,5p'" inside the container.

Phase 3B nginx port test output

lets also consider network security and outside intrusion.

ChatGPT said:
You’ve now got a clean PASS on every Docker foundation check we care about:

Docker core verdict
✅ Outbound HTTPS from containers works (got Example Domain HTML)
✅ Container → host routing works (host.docker.internal:11434 returns version)
✅ Host → container port publishing works (nginx served on 127.0.0.1:8089)
✅ Bridge network + gateway sane (default via 172.17.0.1, subnet 172.17.0.0/16)
✅ DNS sane (nameserver 192.168.65.7)
✅ WSL2 backend + Linux engine confirmed

The BusyBox warning (TLS certificate validation not implemented) is just BusyBox being minimal; it’s not a Docker problem. Your curl-based tests are the “real” TLS validation tests.

Now: harden Docker networking against outside intrusion (systemic + verifiable)
You already did the single best thing: binding to 127.0.0.1 only. Let’s formalize a security baseline with checks.

1) Rule: never publish services on 0.0.0.0 unless you mean it
Good (local-only):

-p 127.0.0.1:8080:8080

Risky (LAN/WAN exposed):

-p 8080:8080 (binds to all interfaces)

Verification: list published ports

docker ps --format "table {{.Names}}\t{{.Image}}\t{{.Ports}}"
Pass: anything sensitive shows 127.0.0.1:...->... not 0.0.0.0:...->....

2) Confirm Windows Firewall isn’t accidentally opening Docker ports
Even if Docker binds to all interfaces, Windows Firewall decides whether inbound is allowed.

Check inbound rules involving Docker:

Get-NetFirewallRule |
  Where-Object { $_.DisplayName -match "Docker" -or $_.DisplayGroup -match "Docker" } |
  Select-Object DisplayName, Enabled, Direction, Action, Profile |
  Format-Table -Auto
Also check what’s actually listening on the host:

Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen |
  Select-Object LocalAddress, LocalPort, OwningProcess |
  Sort-Object LocalPort |
  Format-Table -Auto
Pass: your app ports listen on 127.0.0.1 (or ::1) unless intentionally exposed.

3) Lock down container-to-container exposure (use a private network)
Default bridge lets containers talk to each other. That’s fine, but for “systemic” security, isolate stacks.

Create a dedicated network per app:

docker network create claw_net
Run internal services without publishing ports, only on the private network:

docker run -d --name internal_service --network claw_net someimage
Run only the “edge” service with -p 127.0.0.1:... if you need host access.

Verification

docker network inspect claw_net
4) Make “ingress” explicit: add a reverse proxy (optional but strong)
If you eventually have multiple internal services (Openclaw gateway, UI, etc.), expose only one port locally and route internally (e.g., Caddy/nginx/Traefik). This avoids accidentally publishing multiple ports.

Not required yet—just a pattern.

5) Prevent privilege escalation inside containers (baseline hardening flags)
When you run your Openclaw containers, use these defaults where possible:

--read-only (if the app doesn’t need filesystem writes)

--cap-drop=ALL and add back only what’s needed

--security-opt no-new-privileges:true

--pids-limit 256

--memory / --cpus limits

Run as non-root (--user 1000:1000) if the image supports it

Verification: inspect effective config

docker inspect <container_name> | Select-String -Pattern "ReadonlyRootfs|CapDrop|no-new-privileges|User|PidsLimit|Memory|NanoCpus"
6) Supply chain hygiene (prevent pulling random/untrusted images)
This matters if you’ve been troubleshooting for weeks: it’s easy to run random images/commands.

Prefer official images (nginx, redis, postgres, etc.)

Pin versions (avoid :latest in production-ish setups)

Optionally scan images:

docker scout quickview nginx:alpine
docker scout cves nginx:alpine
7) About host.docker.internal and Ollama exposure
Right now, Ollama is reachable at localhost:11434 on Windows and containers can call it via host.docker.internal:11434.

Security posture:

If Ollama binds only to localhost, it’s not reachable from your LAN.

Verify what address Ollama listens on:

Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen | Where-Object { $_.LocalPort -eq 11434 } | Format-Table -Auto
Pass: LocalAddress is 127.0.0.1 or ::1 (not 0.0.0.0).

If it is listening on 0.0.0.0 and you don’t want LAN access, we’ll lock it down.

Your “Docker security baseline” (short, enforceable)
Only publish ports on 127.0.0.1

Use a dedicated Docker network for the stack

Don’t publish internal services

Drop caps + no-new-privileges + run non-root where possible

Pin images and scan them

Next step
Before you share Openclaw details, run these two verification commands and paste output:

docker ps --format "table {{.Names}}\t{{.Image}}\t{{.Ports}}"
Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen | Where-Object { $_.LocalPort -in 11434,8080,8089 } | Format-Table -Auto
That tells us exactly what is exposed on the host right now, and whether anything is accidentally listening publicly. Then we’ll move to Openclaw and build the same step-by-step, testable deployment plan.

ChatGPT said:
You’re in a really good place on Docker now. Everything you ran up through the “security hardening” section is consistent and sane.

What your outputs tell me (Docker + exposure)
✅ No containers running (docker ps empty)
✅ Ollama is local-only (127.0.0.1:11434) — that’s the big one for intrusion risk
✅ Bridge network is normal (172.17.0.0/16, gateway 172.17.0.1)
✅ Docker Desktop DNS is normal (nameserver 192.168.65.7)
✅ Your new dedicated network claw_net was created correctly (172.18.0.0/16, gateway 172.18.0.1)

The only “stop” at #4 was because someimage was just a placeholder. Not an issue.

Security focus: what is actually exposed to the outside right now?
From your Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen list, the only LLM-related port is 11434 and it’s bound to 127.0.0.1. That means:

Not reachable from LAN/WAN ✅

Only your local machine (and Docker containers via host.docker.internal) can talk to it ✅

That’s already the correct “safe default”.

The ports that are on 0.0.0.0 are Windows services / apps
Examples in your list like 135, 445, lots of 496xx, etc. are typical Windows/RPC/SMB stuff. That’s normal for a Windows box, but if you’re worried about intrusion, you harden that at the Windows firewall/router level, not Docker.

A tighter, verifiable security baseline for your Openclaw stack
1) Make claw_net a true “internal-only” network
Right now your network shows "Internal": false. Set it to internal so containers on it cannot reach the internet, and the internet cannot reach them.

Do this (create a new internal network; you can’t flip the flag on an existing one):

docker network rm claw_net
docker network create --internal claw_net
docker network inspect claw_net
Pass criteria: in inspect output you should see "Internal": true.

Why this matters: it prevents a compromised container from phoning home / pulling payloads, and forces all external access to go through a controlled edge.

2) Only one “edge” container gets a published port (and bind to localhost)
When we run Openclaw later, the pattern should be:

gateway (edge): publishes 127.0.0.1:PORT:PORT

everything else: no -p at all, only --network claw_net

This is the cleanest way to avoid accidental exposure.

3) Add a “no surprises” check before and after starting Openclaw
Run this before you start anything:

docker ps --format "table {{.Names}}\t{{.Image}}\t{{.Ports}}"
Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen | Sort-Object LocalPort | Select LocalAddress,LocalPort,OwningProcess
Then run it again after you start the stack and compare:

Any new listeners should be 127.0.0.1 only (unless explicitly intended).

If something binds to 0.0.0.0, we catch it immediately.

4) Container hardening flags (use on anything you don’t fully trust)
For services that don’t need elevated privileges:

--security-opt no-new-privileges:true

--cap-drop=ALL

--read-only (if possible)

--tmpfs /tmp (if the app needs temp writes)

--user 1000:1000 (if supported)

We’ll apply these selectively once we know Openclaw’s requirements.

One more important observation from your docker info
You have a proxy configured:

HTTP Proxy: http.docker.internal:3128

HTTPS Proxy: http.docker.internal:3128

This can be totally fine, but if that proxy ever misbehaves it can cause “weeks of weirdness” symptoms (pull failures, timeouts, odd DNS-like issues). Keep it in the “known variables” list.

Verification: test a pull and an HTTPS fetch from a container (you already did, both worked). So proxy is not breaking you right now.

Replace the placeholder someimage with a known internal service test
To validate claw_net behavior (before Openclaw), we can run two tiny containers and prove:

they can talk to each other on claw_net

nothing is exposed on the host

and if we make the network --internal, they can’t reach the internet

A) Create internal network (if you do that step)
docker network rm claw_net
docker network create --internal claw_net
B) Start a simple HTTP service inside it (no published ports)
docker run -d --name claw_http --network claw_net nginx:alpine
C) From another container on the same network, curl it
docker run --rm --network claw_net curlimages/curl:latest curl -s http://claw_http
Pass criteria: nginx HTML prints.

D) Confirm host cannot reach it (because no port published)
curl http://127.0.0.1:80
Pass criteria: fails / no service.

E) Confirm internal network blocks internet egress
docker run --rm --network claw_net curlimages/curl:latest curl -s https://example.com
Pass criteria: fails (can’t reach out).

If you run these, we’ll have hard proof that your “internal-only” segmentation works before Openclaw enters the picture.

Next: Openclaw details (what to provide)
When you’re ready, paste either:

your docker compose.yml for Openclaw, or

the exact docker run commands you were using, or

How to read the results (so you know what we’re verifying):

In the docker ps table, we’re looking for Ports that show 0.0.0.0:...->... (exposed to LAN/WAN) vs 127.0.0.1:...->... (local-only).

In the Get-NetTCPConnection list, we’re looking for any new listeners on ports you care about (typically 11434 for Ollama, and whatever Openclaw uses) and whether they bind to 127.0.0.1 vs 0.0.0.0.

If you want, after you paste the output I can also map the OwningProcess PIDs to process names with a single command so you can see exactly what each listener is.

ChatGPT said:
Great—this is the “clean baseline” we want.

Docker exposure baseline
docker ps is empty ✅ → no container ports are published right now.

Ollama is listening only on localhost: 127.0.0.1:11434 ✅ → not reachable from LAN/WAN.

So at this moment, Docker is not exposing anything externally, and your LLM endpoint is local-only.

What’s exposed on 0.0.0.0 (outside-reachable)
You do have several Windows listeners bound to all interfaces:

135 (RPC Endpoint Mapper)

139 / 445 (SMB/NetBIOS)

2179 (often Hyper-V / related services)

5040, 27036, 50217, 57621, and some 496xx dynamic ports

These are Windows/installed-app surface area, not Docker. If your threat model includes “outside intrusion,” these matter more than Docker right now.

Next verification: map PIDs → process names (no guessing)
Run:

Get-Process -Id 2280,4,4016,6876,12884,8300 |
  Select-Object Id,ProcessName,Path |
  Format-Table -Auto
And for the high ports (optional but useful):

Get-Process -Id 8,1940,2688,4244,5976,2040 |
  Select-Object Id,ProcessName,Path |
  Format-Table -Auto
That will tell us exactly what is responsible for each 0.0.0.0 listener.

Security hardening choices (systemic, low drama)
If you want to reduce external exposure without breaking local dev, the two biggest wins are:

Ensure your PC network profile is “Private” only when needed; otherwise Public is stricter.

Block inbound SMB/RPC from non-local subnets unless you explicitly use file sharing / remote admin.

A safe pattern is to keep SMB enabled only for your LAN if you need it; otherwise block 139/445 inbound.

Quick check: what firewall profile is active?
Get-NetConnectionProfile | Format-Table -Auto
Quick check: are SMB ports reachable from another device?
From another machine on your LAN you can test:

\\192.168.1.249\ (File Explorer)
If that works and you don’t want it to, we should block it.

Docker-specific security posture (already strong)
When you later start Openclaw, your rule should be:

publish only with 127.0.0.1:PORT:PORT

put internal services on a dedicated network (--internal if feasible)

no published ports except the one gateway/UI you intend

That keeps Docker from becoming an intrusion vector.

Next: tell me what “outside intrusion” means for you
To pick the right hardening steps, answer with just one of these (no long explanation needed):

A) “Only protect against the internet” (router NAT assumed)

B) “Protect against other devices on my LAN too”

C) “Assume a hostile local user/process on this PC”

In parallel, paste the PID→process mapping outputs above, and then we’ll lock down only what’s necessary (without breaking your dev workflow).

Why this matters for security (so we don’t “break the thing”)
Different stacks need different allowances:

If Openclaw expects containers to reach the internet (plugins, model downloads, telemetry), an --internal network would break that.

If it expects LAN clients (phone, another PC) to hit the gateway, binding to 127.0.0.1 would break that.

If it uses WebSockets / SSE, some reverse proxies / firewall rules need specific settings.

So we’ll build a service graph first, then apply security as constraints with a verification test after each change.

While you gather Openclaw details: quick safe defaults that won’t interfere
These are “almost never harmful”:

Keep binding published ports to 127.0.0.1 for now (local-only) until you explicitly want LAN access.

Don’t change firewall rules yet.

Don’t switch to --internal networks yet.

network/volumes created ✅

gateway health endpoint ✅

orchestrator internal DNS ✅

Ollama reachability ✅

end-to-end “reasoning” smoke test ✅

only then: tighten exposure with one controlled change at a time ✅

Thought for 8m 54s
Perfect — sharing those docs before we harden anything is exactly the right move.

From the OpenClaw “Docker” doc in your zip, there are two distinct Docker uses you’ll want to keep separate in your mental model:

Containerized Gateway (full OpenClaw in Docker Compose)

Per-session Agent Sandbox (host gateway + Docker-isolated tool runs)

The doc explicitly says Docker is optional and is mainly for a containerized gateway or validating the Docker flow, and it points to a Docker Compose “quick start” using ./docker-setup.sh, which builds the gateway image, runs onboarding, starts via compose, generates a token into .env, then you open the Control UI at http://127.0.0.1:18789/ and paste the token. (That matches the OCR I pulled from your openclaw6.pdf.)

Given your last two weeks, here’s the systemic approach with verifications at every step, designed so later security changes don’t silently break Discord or the gateway.

Phase A — Decide the topology (before any security knobs)
Topology 1 (recommended to start stable)
Ollama runs on Windows (127.0.0.1:11434) ✅ you already have this

OpenClaw Gateway runs in Docker Compose

Gateway container calls Ollama via http://host.docker.internal:11434

This is usually the least moving parts on Windows.

Verification checkpoint
From a container:

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags
Pass = you see gemma3:12b.

Phase B — Bring up OpenClaw gateway in Docker (compose)
From the doc: from repo root run:

./docker-setup.sh (quick start, recommended)
and it ends with Control UI at http://127.0.0.1:18789/, token pasted in Settings.

B1) Pre-flight verification (no containers yet)
docker ps
Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen | Where-Object { $_.LocalPort -in 18789,11434 } | Format-Table -Auto
Pass = nothing on 18789 yet; Ollama still only on 127.0.0.1:11434.

B2) Run the gateway setup script
(From the OpenClaw repo root)

./docker-setup.sh
B3) Verify compose state deterministically
docker compose ps
docker compose logs --tail 200
Pass = gateway service is “Up/healthy” (or at least “Up”), and logs show it started cleanly.

B4) Verify the Control UI is reachable locally
curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18789/
Pass = HTTP 200/302/304 (anything that proves it’s responding).

B5) Verify gateway ➜ Ollama connectivity (critical)
Run a container-side call to Ollama and a gateway-side “generate” test.

You already proved container ➜ host works, so the key is: does the gateway container use the right base URL?

A clean way to verify from inside the running gateway container:

docker exec -it <gateway_container_name> sh -lc 'curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/version'
Pass = version JSON.

(If the container doesn’t have curl, we’ll use a throwaway container on the same network to test the gateway’s egress path.)

Phase C — Add Discord as the first channel (but keep it isolated)
You said: start with Discord, later replace with a custom chat UI. That’s great because Discord becomes an integration test harness for the full pipeline.

At a high level, Discord integration always has the same verification ladder:

Discord bot can connect (token + intents ok)

Discord events reach your gateway/connector

Gateway can call the LLM (Ollama)

Response returns back to Discord

C1) Discord-only verification (before involving OpenClaw)
Create Discord application + bot

Add bot to your server

Confirm it can come online (logs will show “connected”)

C2) OpenClaw Discord connector verification
When you wire Discord into OpenClaw, we’ll enforce these checkpoints:

Container logs show the bot logged in successfully

A trivial command like !ping (or whatever OpenClaw uses) returns a deterministic response

Then an LLM request returns a response

Important for later security hardening:
Discord requires outbound internet access from whichever container runs the bot/connector. So if we later make a Docker network --internal, Discord will break. That’s why we’ll postpone “no egress” hardening until after Discord is stable, and then we’ll harden in a controlled way (e.g., only internalize non-Discord services).

Phase D — Security changes that won’t surprise you
Once OpenClaw + Discord work, we harden with one change at a time, each with the same 4 checks:

docker compose ps

Control UI reachable (curl http://127.0.0.1:18789/)

Gateway can reach Ollama

Discord message round-trip works

We’ll start with the safest security constraint:

Bind published ports to localhost only (127.0.0.1:...)
You’re already aligned with this approach.

We will not add --internal networks until we confirm which services must reach Discord and which must reach package repos, etc.

your OpenClaw docker-compose.yml (or the compose files the setup script generates), or

the repo structure + the services it starts (service names from docker compose ps after setup)

ChatGPT said:
You’re not stuck on 4b — what you’re seeing in Explorer is just the manifest files (tiny pointers), not the actual model weights.

Why those files are only ~1 KB
Ollama stores models like this:

Manifests (small JSON pointers):
C:\Users\PLUTO\.ollama\models\manifests\registry.ollama.ai\library\gemma3\{tag}
That’s what you’re looking at in the screenshot (4b and 12b, both ~1 KB).

Actual weights (“blobs”) live here (big files):
C:\Users\PLUTO\.ollama\models\blobs\
These are keyed by digest (sha256…).

Your /api/tags output already proves 12b is present locally:

gemma3:12b size ~8.15 GB and digest f4031aab... ✅

Verify the 12b weights exist on disk (Windows host)
Run this in PowerShell:

# confirm the blob file for gemma3:12b exists and show its size
$dig = "sha256-f4031aab637d1ffa37b42570452ae0e4fad0314754d17ded67322e4b95836f8a"
Get-Item "$env:USERPROFILE\.ollama\models\blobs\$dig" | Select Name, Length
Pass criteria: Length is in the billions of bytes (~8GB range).

If that exact file name doesn’t exist (rare formatting differences), list blobs sorted by size:

Get-ChildItem "$env:USERPROFILE\.ollama\models\blobs" |
  Sort-Object Length -Descending |
  Select-Object -First 10 Name, Length
You should see at least one very large blob that matches the 12b weight size.

Verify Ollama can serve 12b (you already did, but here’s the “hard proof”)
Host (Windows):

ollama run gemma3:12b "Reply with: USING 12B"
From a container (you already got this working):

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/generate `
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" `
  -d '{"model":"gemma3:12b","prompt":"Reply with USING 12B","stream":false}'
Pass criteria: response includes USING 12B.

The real place you can get “stuck on 4b”
Not Ollama — your app (Openclaw) config may default to gemma3:4b.

When we wire Openclaw, we’ll add a verification step that checks exactly what model it requested. Typical ways:

Explicit env var in compose (example patterns)

MODEL=gemma3:12b or OLLAMA_MODEL=gemma3:12b

Openclaw config file specifying the model name.

Logs: most gateways log the model string they call.

When you paste your Openclaw compose/config, I’ll point to the exact knob.

Next step (fastest)
Run the blob-size check command above and paste the output. That will definitively confirm the 12b weights are on disk and not just a manifest pointer.

ChatGPT said:
You’re 100% using gemma3:12b already:

ollama run gemma3:12b "USING 12B" returned USING 12B ✅

The container call to /api/generate returned "model":"gemma3:12b" ✅

/api/tags reports gemma3:12b with size 8149190253 (~8.15 GB) ✅

So it’s not “stuck on 4b” at runtime.

Why your blob file lookup failed
That’s just a filename mismatch. Ollama’s digest field is a content hash, but the blob file on disk might be stored as:

sha256-<hash> or

sha256:<hash> or

just <hash>

…depending on the Ollama build/platform and how the storage layer is implemented.

So instead of guessing the exact filename, we’ll find it.

Find the 12b blob on disk (robust commands)
1) Confirm the blobs folder exists
Test-Path "$env:USERPROFILE\.ollama\models\blobs"
Should return True.

2) List the biggest blobs (the 12b one will be near the top)
Get-ChildItem "$env:USERPROFILE\.ollama\models\blobs" -File |
  Sort-Object Length -Descending |
  Select-Object -First 10 Name, Length
Pass criteria: you see one around 8+ GB (that’s your 12b weights).

3) Search by digest “hash portion” (most precise)
Your digest from /api/tags is:

f4031aab637d1ffa37b42570452ae0e4fad0314754d17ded67322e4b95836f8a

Search for any blob containing that substring:

$h = "f4031aab637d1ffa37b42570452ae0e4fad0314754d17ded67322e4b95836f8a"
Get-ChildItem "$env:USERPROFILE\.ollama\models\blobs" -File |
  Where-Object { $_.Name -match $h } |
  Select-Object Name, Length
If this returns nothing, it usually means the blob filename is not that hash string (still fine). In that case, the “biggest blobs” method above is the right verification.

Extra-strong verification: prove the manifest points to a big blob
Since you were looking at:
...\manifests\registry.ollama.ai\library\gemma3\12b

Let’s print that manifest and see what blob(s) it references:

$mf = "$env:USERPROFILE\.ollama\models\manifests\registry.ollama.ai\library\gemma3\12b"
Get-Content $mf -Raw
You’ll typically see references to one or more sha256:... layer digests. Then you can search those digests in the blobs directory.

Bottom line
Your system is not stuck on gemma3:4b.

Explorer shows tiny manifest files for 4b and 12b (expected).

The real weights are in ...\.ollama\models\blobs\ and we can identify them by size.

If you paste the output of the “top 10 biggest blobs” command, I’ll point to the exact file that corresponds to 12b and we can lock that into your step-by-step Openclaw verification runbook.

Explorer showing 1 KB files under manifests/.../gemma3/{4b,12b} is normal.

Why /api/tags digest didn’t match the blob filename you tried
/api/tags gave you a model digest like f4031aab..., but the manifest shows the actual model layer digest is e8ad13.... That’s also normal:

The tag digest often represents the model reference / overall model identity

The manifest’s layers[].digest are the actual stored blobs (weights, template, license, params)

That’s why searching blobs for f4031aab... returned nothing, but you still have the right 8GB blob.

Sure — let’s check whether OpenClaw is already on your system before we clone anything. We’ll do this in a way that’s fast and unambiguous.

1) Check common folders for an openclaw repo
Run these in PowerShell:

# Common places people keep repos
$roots = @(
  "$env:USERPROFILE",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\Documents",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\Desktop",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\source",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\repos",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\git",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\github"
)

$roots | ForEach-Object {
  if (Test-Path $_) {
    Get-ChildItem $_ -Directory -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue |
      Where-Object { $_.Name -match "openclaw" } |
      Select-Object FullName
  }
}
If anything prints a path, we’ll inspect that folder next.

2) Search for “docker-setup.sh” or “docker-compose.yml” containing “openclaw”
This catches cases where the folder name isn’t “openclaw”.

# Search likely dev folders for OpenClaw's docker entrypoints
$searchRoots = @(
  "$env:USERPROFILE\source",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\repos",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\git",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\github",
  "$env:USERPROFILE\Documents"
)

$searchRoots | ForEach-Object {
  if (Test-Path $_) {
    Get-ChildItem $_ -Recurse -File -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue |
      Where-Object { $_.Name -in @("docker-setup.sh","docker-compose.yml","compose.yml") } |
      Where-Object { $_.FullName -match "openclaw" } |
      Select-Object -First 20 FullName
  }
}
3) If you installed something via pip (Python) rather than cloning
Check if a Python package named like openclaw exists:

python --version
python -m pip show openclaw 2>$null
If that prints package info, we’ll treat it as “installed,” but it’s usually still better to use the repo for Docker Compose workflows.

4) If you used WSL (Ubuntu) for dev, check there too
In PowerShell:

wsl -d Ubuntu -- bash -lc 'ls -la ~; find ~ -maxdepth 3 -type d -iname "*openclaw*" 2>/dev/null | head -n 20'

ChatGPT said:
Nice — that docker-compose.yml tells us a lot.

Key findings from your compose
This compose file doesn’t configure the LLM at all.
It only starts the gateway and a CLI container. No Ollama vars, no model vars.

Your gateway is started with:

gateway --bind ${OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_BIND:-lan} --port 18789

That default lan means it may bind to a LAN interface (more exposure than 127.0.0.1). We can keep it lan for now if you need Discord access externally later, but for safest local testing we’ll set it to local.

The actual model choice (“gemma3:4b vs gemma3:12b”) is almost certainly stored in the mounted config directory:

${OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR}:/home/node/.openclaw

So the UI you showed is reading a config file under OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR (on your Windows filesystem).

Fix your repo-wide search command (PowerShell syntax issue)
You hit: A parameter cannot be found that matches parameter name 'Recurse'.

That happens when PowerShell is treating Select-String differently than expected (usually due to the way -Path .\* is used). The reliable pattern is: get files first, then pipe into Select-String.

Run this:

$patterns = @(
  "ollama",
  "host.docker.internal",
  "11434",
  "gemma",
  "model",
  "llm",
  "provider",
  "OPENAI_BASE_URL",
  "OPENAI_API_BASE",
  "OPENAI_API_KEY",
  "LOCALAI",
  "LITELLM",
  "gateway"
)

Get-ChildItem . -Recurse -File |
  Select-String -Pattern $patterns -SimpleMatch -CaseSensitive:$false |
  Select-Object -First 120
Paste the first chunk of output.

That will tell us exactly where OpenClaw stores model/provider settings (likely in docs, config templates, or gateway code).

Meanwhile: the most likely reason you “see 4b” in the UI
OpenClaw probably ships with a default model preset (e.g. gemma3:4b) in its config folder on first run.

Since the config folder is mounted from your host, changing containers won’t change that default unless you edit the config.

First: identify what OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR is currently set to
If you don’t have a .env yet, docker-setup.sh may create it. Let’s check what you have:

Test-Path .\.env
if (Test-Path .\.env) { Get-Content .\.env }
Also show the value if it’s set in your shell:

echo $env:OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR
Next “systemic” verification: locate the config files on Windows
Once you know OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR, list it:

$cfg = $env:OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR
if (-not $cfg -or -not (Test-Path $cfg)) {
  Write-Host "OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR not set or path missing: $cfg"
} else {
  Get-ChildItem $cfg -Recurse -File | Select FullName
}
We’re looking for files that define providers/models—often named like:

config.json

settings.json

providers.json

models.json

gateway.json

YAML/TOML equivalents

Once we find them, we can pin:

provider = Ollama

base URL = http://host.docker.internal:11434

model = gemma3:12b

…and then verify by making OpenClaw issue a request and confirming "model":"gemma3:12b" in the Ollama API call.

Quick safety note (since you mentioned security)
Your compose defaults OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_BIND to lan. For local-only testing, set it to local (or whatever OpenClaw expects for localhost-only).

We’ll do that later after we have it working, unless you want to lock it down immediately.

ChatGPT said:

Thought for 9s
Good — this explains the “stuck” feeling:

You haven’t created a .env, so Compose has no values for required variables like OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR, OPENCLAW_WORKSPACE_DIR, OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN, etc.

In your docker-compose.yml, those vars are not optional (e.g., ${OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR}:/home/node/.openclaw). If they’re blank, Docker will either error or mount something unintended.

The model/provider choice (Ollama + gemma3:12b vs gemma3:4b) is controlled by files under /home/node/.openclaw, which will map to whatever OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR points to on Windows.

So: next step is to create a clean, explicit .env + config directory, then run the setup, then set Ollama provider/model.

Step 1 — Create a deterministic .env (Windows-safe)
From C:\Users\PLUTO\github\openclaw:

Copy-Item .\.env.example .\.env -Force
notepad .\.env
Now put in (or edit) these lines in .env (you can paste at bottom if similar keys exist already):

# --- Docker/Compose basics ---
OPENCLAW_IMAGE=openclaw:local

# Put OpenClaw config + workspace somewhere explicit on Windows
OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR=C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config
OPENCLAW_WORKSPACE_DIR=C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_workspace

# Make gateway local-only while we stabilize (safer than LAN)
OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_BIND=loopback

# Ports (keep defaults unless you need to change)
OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=18789
OPENCLAW_BRIDGE_PORT=18790

# Auth token for the Control UI / clients
OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN=change-me-to-a-long-random-token
Generate a good token quickly:

# Generates a strong token; copy it into OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN
[Convert]::ToBase64String((1..48 | ForEach-Object {Get-Random -Max 256}))
Create the directories you referenced:

mkdir C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config -Force
mkdir C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_workspace -Force

Step 3 — Configure Ollama + gemma3:12b (where your “4b vs 12b” actually gets decided)
OpenClaw keeps its settings under the mounted config dir:

C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config ⇄ /home/node/.openclaw

Two ways to set the model:

Option A: Set it in the Control UI (recommended)
Open http://127.0.0.1:18789/

In Settings / Providers:

Provider: Ollama

Base URL: http://host.docker.internal:11434

Model: gemma3:12b

Option B: Edit the config file directly (fully deterministic)
First, list what OpenClaw wrote into your config dir:

Get-ChildItem C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config -Recurse -File | Select FullName
We’re looking for something like openclaw.json or similar. Once you see it, we’ll edit the exact keys (OpenClaw documents them in docs/providers/ollama.md and docs/gateway/configuration-reference.md in the repo).

Step 4 — Prove OpenClaw is calling gemma3:12b (no guessing)
After you set Ollama+model in UI/config, run this (it watches gateway logs for model/provider hints):

docker compose logs -f | Select-String -Pattern "ollama|gemma3:12b|model" -CaseSensitive:$false
And in parallel, run an Ollama request from a container (already proven working):

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest `
  curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/generate `
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" `
  -d '{"model":"gemma3:12b","prompt":"Reply with USING 12B","stream":false}'
If OpenClaw is still using 4b after you set it, that means the OpenClaw config didn’t persist (wrong config dir mount) — and the mount is exactly what .env fixes.

Quick note on OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_BIND
Your compose defaults to lan. For “extremely careful” bring-up, use loopback (local-only) until Discord is working, then switch to LAN if you truly need other devices to hit the gateway.

ChatGPT said:
Mostly yes — spaces are fine (dotenv ignores whitespace-only lines and treats lines starting with # as comments). But there are two important corrections for Windows + Docker Compose:

1) Don’t define OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN twice
Right now you have it at the top and again at the bottom. Most dotenv loaders take the last one wins (and some behave differently), so this can cause confusion.

Fix: keep only one OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN= line. Put it near your Docker section and delete/comment the earlier one.

Example:

# Auth token for the Control UI / clients
OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN=<your-long-random-token>
2) Windows paths in .env: use forward slashes (recommended)
Docker Compose env parsing + Windows paths can get weird. The safest pattern is:

C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_config

C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_workspace

So change:

OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR=C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config
OPENCLAW_WORKSPACE_DIR=C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_workspace
to:

OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR=C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_config
OPENCLAW_WORKSPACE_DIR=C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_workspace
This avoids backslash escaping edge cases.

3) OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_BIND=loopback is the right idea, but verify it’s a valid value
Your docker-compose.yml defaults to lan. Some apps use loopback, some use localhost, some use local. We’ll verify quickly after start-up by checking what it binds to.

If loopback isn’t recognized, it might fail to start or still bind to LAN. We’ll catch that immediately in logs.

Clean minimal .env block for your case
If you want the least moving parts, your bottom block should look like:

OPENCLAW_IMAGE=openclaw:local

OPENCLAW_CONFIG_DIR=C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_config
OPENCLAW_WORKSPACE_DIR=C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_workspace

OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_BIND=loopback
OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=18789
OPENCLAW_BRIDGE_PORT=18790

OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN=<your-long-random-token>
Everything else can stay commented until you actually need it (Discord token, etc.).

Verification (no guessing)
After saving .env, run:

docker compose config
This prints the fully-resolved compose file. You should see your volumes expanded to the Windows paths and your env vars present. If the paths/token aren’t showing up there, we fix it before running anything.

If you paste the output of docker compose config (just the parts showing volumes: and environment: for openclaw-gateway), I’ll confirm it’s correct.

Gateway ports are published:

18789 and 18790 are published (we’ll check they bind to localhost after start)

The warnings are fine

Next step: build + start the gateway (with checkpoints)
From the same folder:

docker compose up -d
docker compose ps
docker compose logs --tail 200
Then verify it’s reachable:

curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18789/
Security verification (important)
After it’s up, confirm the port is only local:

Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen |
  Where-Object { $_.LocalPort -in 18789,18790 } |
  Format-Table -Auto
Pass criteria: LocalAddress should be 127.0.0.1 (or ::1), not 0.0.0.0.

After it’s running: we pin it to Ollama + gemma3:12b
Since Compose doesn’t set model/provider, OpenClaw will store that in the config dir you mounted:
C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config

Once the gateway has started at least once, run:

Get-ChildItem C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config -Recurse -File | Select FullName
Then we’ll either:

set Ollama + http://host.docker.internal:11434 + gemma3:12b in the Control UI, or

edit the config file directly (most deterministic).

ChatGPT said:
You did everything right up to the point of starting—the failure is simple and deterministic:

Error response from daemon: pull access denied for openclaw, repository does not exist…

That means the image openclaw:local does not exist yet, and Docker tried (and failed) to pull it from a registry.

So the next step is build the image locally (one time), then docker compose up -d will work.

Also: the repeated Claude warnings are harmless.

0) Reset to a clean slate (no config churn)
From C:\Users\PLUTO\github\openclaw:

docker compose down --remove-orphans
docker image ls | Select-String -Pattern "openclaw" -CaseSensitive:$false
This just confirms there’s no running stack and no existing image.

1) Build the local image (this is the missing piece)
Run:

docker build -t openclaw:local .
Verification checkpoint

docker image ls openclaw:local
You should see openclaw local ...

If the build fails, paste the last ~80 lines of output and we’ll fix it (usually node/pnpm build steps).

2) Bring up the gateway (same command as before)
docker compose up -d
docker compose ps
docker compose logs --tail 200
Verification

curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18789/
Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen | Where-Object { $_.LocalPort -in 18789,18790 } | Format-Table -Auto
Pass = curl returns headers and port(s) are listening (ideally on localhost).

3) Prevent the “it tried to pull from Docker Hub” issue forever
Two safe options:

Option A (keep what you have): always build first
You’re already using OPENCLAW_IMAGE=openclaw:local. That’s fine—just build once.

Option B (make compose build automatically)
Edit docker-compose.yml so the gateway service has a build: stanza. But since you want minimal file changes, Option A is best right now.

Why you got into “cycles” before
This is a classic trap: env/config changes don’t matter until the image exists and the service is running. Then people start changing unrelated configs trying to fix a “connection refused” that’s actually “container never started.”

Right now, your state is clean:

No container is running (hence curl fails).

No listener on 18789/18790 (hence Get-NetTCPConnection shows nothing).

Root cause is image missing.

Do this next (exact order)
docker build -t openclaw:local .

docker image ls openclaw:local

docker compose up -d

Paste:

the output of docker image ls openclaw:local

and docker compose ps

Then we’ll move to the next verification stage (gateway reachable → configure Ollama base URL → pin gemma3:12b → Discord).

ChatGPT said:
You’re doing great—this is exactly the kind of “checkpointed” bring-up that prevents spirals.

Right now we have two concrete issues (both fixable without random edits):

Ports are published on 0.0.0.0 even though you set --bind loopback

curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18789/ returns “Empty reply from server” (meaning something accepted the TCP connection but didn’t return HTTP headers)

Let’s diagnose first, then make the smallest safe change.

A) Diagnose the “empty reply” precisely
A1) Confirm the gateway process is actually running and healthy
Run:

docker compose logs openclaw-gateway --tail 200
If you see a stack trace or “listening on …” line, that will tell us what protocol it’s speaking.

A2) Check the container’s own health from inside its network namespace
This avoids Windows/port-forward weirdness.

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "ss -ltnp || netstat -ltnp"
Then try curling from inside the container:

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 30"
Interpretation:

If the container itself also gets “empty reply” or doesn’t respond, the gateway may not be serving plain HTTP at / (could be websocket/SSE-only, or expecting a specific path).

If container curl works but host curl doesn’t, it’s a port publishing / bind / Windows networking issue.

A3) Try the other port
Your compose publishes 18790 too (bridge). Sometimes the UI is on one and API on the other.

curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18790/
B) Fix the “0.0.0.0 published” exposure (minimal + reversible)
This is purely Docker publishing behavior: even if the app binds loopback internally, Docker is still exposing host ports on all interfaces.

The safest move: bind published ports to 127.0.0.1 at the Docker level.

You don’t need to edit files to test this; just do it via .env overrides since your compose already uses env vars for ports.

B1) Change .env ports to include host bind address
Edit .env and set:

OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=127.0.0.1:18789
OPENCLAW_BRIDGE_PORT=127.0.0.1:18790
That works because your compose line is:

"${OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT:-18789}:18789"

So the left side can be 127.0.0.1:18789.

B2) Apply cleanly
docker compose down
docker compose up -d
docker compose ps
B3) Verify exposure is now local-only
Get-NetTCPConnection -State Listen |
  Where-Object { $_.LocalPort -in 18789,18790 } |
  Format-Table -Auto
Pass criteria: LocalAddress becomes 127.0.0.1 / ::1 only (no :: / no 0.0.0.0).

C) Why you’re seeing :: listeners
Your output shows listeners on :: (IPv6 all interfaces), which effectively exposes the port widely (IPv6). Binding Docker publishes to 127.0.0.1 addresses this.

Do these next (in order) and paste outputs
Gateway logs:

docker compose logs openclaw-gateway --tail 200
Container internal listen + internal HTTP test:

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "ss -ltnp || netstat -ltnp"
docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 30"
Quick host test of the other port:

curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18790/
After we confirm whether the gateway speaks HTTP at / (or needs a specific path), we’ll fix the binding exposure with the .env port tweak above—one small change, fully reversible, no config churn.

ChatGPT said:
This is actually great news because the logs finally give a single clear root cause:

Missing config. Run \openclaw setup` or set gateway.mode=local (or pass --allow-unconfigured).`

Your gateway container is crash-looping because the config directory you mounted (C:/Users/PLUTO/openclaw_config) is empty (fresh), and the gateway refuses to start without initial setup.

That also explains:

curl “empty reply” (container accepts then restarts)

docker exec fails (“container is restarting”)

port 18790 not reachable (service never comes up)

So we do one deterministic thing: run the OpenClaw setup once to generate config into your mounted config dir.

Step 1 — Run openclaw setup in the CLI container
Use the openclaw-cli service to run the setup in the same mounted config dir (so it writes files to C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config).

docker compose run --rm openclaw-cli setup
This should create config files under C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config.

Verification immediately after:

Get-ChildItem C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config -Recurse -File | Select FullName
You should now see at least one config file (often openclaw.json or similar).

Step 2 — Restart gateway cleanly
docker compose up -d
docker compose ps
docker compose logs openclaw-gateway --tail 80
Verification:

curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18789/
Step 3 — Fix the exposure (0.0.0.0) safely, using only .env
Your compose publishes ports as "${OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT:-18789}:18789", so we can make it localhost-only without editing YAML:

Edit .env and set:

OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=127.0.0.1:18789
OPENCLAW_BRIDGE_PORT=127.0.0.1:18790
Apply:

docker compose down
docker compose up -d

ChatGPT said:
Good news: config exists now (we see openclaw.json), so we’re past the original crash-loop problem.

The remaining issue is specific:

Container is “Up”

Host TCP port is open

But curl -I http://127.0.0.1:18789/ gets Empty reply from server

That almost always means one of these is true:

the gateway is not serving plain HTTP on / (it may expect a specific path, or it’s a WS/SSE endpoint), or

the gateway is speaking HTTP/2 / h2c / websocket handshake that curl -I doesn’t handle the way we expect, or

it’s still not fully configured for the “UI” mode and closes the connection immediately, or

something is binding but immediately closing (proxying issue).

We can resolve this without changing files by doing two targeted checks.

1) Get the gateway’s actual startup logs (the ones we need)
Your docker compose logs ... --tail 120 didn’t show the gateway output this time (only Compose warnings). Let’s pull only the container’s logs:

docker logs --tail 200 openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1
What we’re looking for: a line like “listening on …”, “UI available at …”, or a hint about the correct URL path.

2) Test from inside the container (removes Windows networking from the equation)
Since the container is now “Up”, docker exec should work:

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc 'ss -ltnp || netstat -ltnp'
Then try a verbose HTTP request from inside:

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc 'wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 60'
If / isn’t the right path, this will usually still reveal something (redirect, 404, or a clue in headers). If it instantly closes, we’ll see that too.

3) Verify whether the gateway expects auth on HTTP requests
You’ve set OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN. Some gateways require it even for basic endpoints (or require it for UI/API). Let’s try sending it as a header.

First, confirm header name by searching docs quickly locally:

Select-String -Path .\docs\**\*.md -Pattern "OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN|Authorization|Bearer|X-.*TOKEN|gateway token" -CaseSensitive:$false | Select-Object -First 40
But we can also brute-force the common pattern:

curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/ -H "Authorization: Bearer 5722a2376c14437661e69cef616b3bdd03689d0b121e5461"
(Replace token if needed.)

Even if it’s wrong, -v will show whether the server responds with 401/403 vs closing immediately.

4) Lock down the published ports (we’ll do this after it responds)
You’re currently still publishing on 0.0.0.0 because the compose file uses OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=18789 not 127.0.0.1:18789. We can fix that later with a single .env change (no YAML edits), but first we want a working response.

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc 'wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 60'
Host verbose curl:

curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/
Once I see those, I’ll tell you the exact correct URL/path (or auth header) to hit, and we’ll get a clean HTTP 200/302 before touching any more config.

ChatGPT said:
Excellent catch stopping there — the logs show the real blocker now:

“Gateway start blocked: set gateway.mode=local (current: unset) or pass --allow-unconfigured.”

So even though openclaw setup created openclaw.json, it did not set gateway.mode. The gateway will keep crash-looping until we set that one key.

We’ll do this in the least invasive, most reversible way, with a backup and a verification after.

Step 1) Back up your config (one-time safety net)
Copy-Item C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config\openclaw.json C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config\openclaw.json.bak -Force
Verify:

Get-Item C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config\openclaw.json* | Select Name, Length, LastWriteTime
Step 2) Set gateway.mode=local (single-key edit)
We’ll edit JSON in-place safely using PowerShell (no manual Notepad mistakes). This sets only gateway.mode and leaves everything else intact.

$path = "C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config\openclaw.json"
$j = Get-Content $path -Raw | ConvertFrom-Json

if (-not $j.gateway) { $j | Add-Member -NotePropertyName gateway -NotePropertyValue (@{}) }
if (-not $j.gateway.mode) { $j.gateway | Add-Member -NotePropertyName mode -NotePropertyValue "local" -Force } else { $j.gateway.mode = "local" }

# Write back as JSON (pretty)
$j | ConvertTo-Json -Depth 20 | Set-Content -Path $path -Encoding UTF8
Verification checkpoint

(Get-Content C:\Users\PLUTO\openclaw_config\openclaw.json -Raw) | Select-String '"mode"\s*:\s*"local"' -AllMatches
If that prints a match, the key is set.

Step 3) Restart the gateway and confirm it stops restarting
docker compose down
docker compose up -d
docker compose ps
docker logs --tail 120 openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1
Pass criteria

docker compose ps shows gateway Up and not restarting

logs no longer show “Gateway start blocked…”

One config file, one key, backed up.

ChatGPT said:
Right now you’re trying to resolve one specific, root cause problem, and everything else you’re seeing is just a symptom of it.

What we’re actually fixing
The gateway is not starting
Your gateway logs say:

“Gateway start blocked: set gateway.mode=local (current: unset) or pass --allow-unconfigured.”

Because the gateway never fully starts, you get:

curl: (52) Empty reply from server (it accepts a connection, then dies / closes)

docker exec … is restarting (container keeps restarting)

UI never becomes reachable

So the immediate goal is:

✅ Get the gateway to start and stay up (no restart loop).
Only after that does it make sense to configure Ollama + gemma3:12b, Discord, security, etc.

Why “openclaw setup” wasn’t enough
openclaw setup created openclaw.json, but it didn’t set the required key gateway.mode. The gateway refuses to start until that’s set (or you pass --allow-unconfigured).

Two clean ways to unblock it (pick one)
Option A (recommended): set gateway.mode=local in the config
This is the stable, intended fix.

Option B (temporary): start gateway with --allow-unconfigured
Good for quick testing, but you’ll still need real config for Discord and model providers.

ChatGPT said:
Good — you got Notepad++ opened via the full path, and the gateway container is now staying Up (not restarting). That means the gateway.mode fix likely worked.

So why are you still getting “Empty reply from server”?

At this point, that error usually means you’re hitting the wrong kind of endpoint with curl -I. The gateway may:

not serve a normal HTTP page at / (could be websockets/SSE/API only), or

require auth and closes the connection for unauthenticated requests, or

serve UI on a different path/port.

Let’s stop guessing and do 3 deterministic checks.

1) Confirm the gateway is no longer “blocked” in logs
Run:

docker logs --tail 200 openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1
We want to see any “listening on …” or route info. If it still says “start blocked,” then openclaw.json didn’t save properly.

2) Exec into the container and test from inside
Now that it’s Up, this should work:

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "ss -ltnp || netstat -ltnp"
Then request from inside (this avoids Windows/Docker port forwarding weirdness):

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 80"
This will tell us whether:

it returns 200/302/404,

it requires auth,

or it closes immediately.

3) Try the likely “UI” and “health” paths
Many gateways don’t serve anything useful at /. Try these:

curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/
curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/health
curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/api/health
curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/ui
If any returns a real HTTP status line, we’ve found the right surface.

4) Auth test (very likely)
You have a gateway token. It may require it.

Try the common pattern:

curl -v http://127.0.0.1:18789/ -H "Authorization: Bearer 5722a2376c14437661e69cef616b3bdd03689d0b121e5461"
If you get a 401/403 instead of “empty reply,” that’s a win: it proves the server is responding and we just need the correct auth header/path.

Security note (we’ll do after functionality)
You’re still publishing 0.0.0.0:18789-18790. Once we confirm the correct endpoint, we’ll lock it to localhost by changing only .env:

OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=127.0.0.1:18789

OPENCLAW_BRIDGE_PORT=127.0.0.1:18790

Paste back these outputs (in order)
docker logs --tail 200 openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1

docker exec … ss/netstat output

docker exec … wget … output

With that, I’ll tell you exactly what URL to open in your browser and how to authenticate—and we’ll move on to pinning Ollama to gemma3:12b.

That’s why PowerShell curl -I shows “Empty reply”: PowerShell’s curl is often Invoke-WebRequest-ish / quirky with headers-only requests, and this gateway expects browser behavior (HTTP GET for the UI + WebSocket upgrades for the app).

Hard proof it works: inside the container, a normal HTTP GET to / returns HTTP/1.1 200 OK and the HTML for OpenClaw Control. ✅

So the gateway is not broken anymore.

1) Use the right “human” test
Open this in a browser on Windows:

http://127.0.0.1:18789/

You should see OpenClaw Control load.

If it asks for a token, use the one in your .env (OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_TOKEN=...).

2) Fix the “curl empty reply” confusion (optional)
If you want a CLI test that behaves like the container’s wget:

docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 20"
Or on Windows host, use PowerShell’s web request instead of curl -I:

(Invoke-WebRequest -UseBasicParsing http://127.0.0.1:18789/).StatusCode
3) You are currently configured to Anthropic, not Ollama (this is your next real issue)
Your logs say:

[gateway] agent model: anthropic/claude-opus-4-6

That means OpenClaw is defaulting to an Anthropic model right now, not gemma3:12b.

So the next goal is:

✅ Configure OpenClaw provider = Ollama
✅ Base URL = http://host.docker.internal:11434
✅ Model = gemma3:12b

We’ll do this in the Control UI first (least file editing), then we’ll verify via an Ollama API call.

4) Security note: you’re still exposed on LAN right now
Compose shows:

0.0.0.0:18789-18790->...

Even though OpenClaw binds loopback internally, Docker is publishing to all interfaces.

Once you confirm the UI loads, lock it down by changing only .env:

OPENCLAW_GATEWAY_PORT=127.0.0.1:18789
OPENCLAW_BRIDGE_PORT=127.0.0.1:18790
Then:

docker compose down
docker compose up -d
docker compose ps
What to do next (no file churn)
Open http://127.0.0.1:18789/ in your browser.

In Settings → Providers (or Models), set:

Provider: Ollama

URL: http://host.docker.internal:11434

Model: gemma3:12b

Then run this verification from a container (it should continue to show gemma3:12b exists):

docker run --rm curlimages/curl:latest curl -s http://host.docker.internal:11434/api/tags
If you tell me what you see in the Control UI (a screenshot or the exact labels), I’ll tell you exactly which dropdowns/fields to change and what to expect in logs once it switches away from Anthropic to Ollama.

Why curl -I is lying to you
In PowerShell, curl is often an alias / behaves differently than Linux curl (even in Docker Desktop terminal). The key proof is:

Inside the container, wget http://127.0.0.1:18789/ returns HTTP/1.1 200 OK and the Control UI HTML ✅

So the gateway is healthy.

Use this instead (works reliably in your setup)

(Invoke-WebRequest -UseBasicParsing http://127.0.0.1:18789/).StatusCode

docker run --rm --network openclaw_default curlimages/curl:latest -i http://openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1:18789/

ChatGPT said:
You’ve already proven the gateway is serving HTTP correctly (the in-container wget gets HTTP/1.1 200 OK). So the remaining weirdness is all about where you’re making the request from and how Docker Desktop/PowerShell is handling it.

Also: your last docker run --network openclaw_default … openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1:18789 failed because you recreated the network when you did docker compose down (it removes openclaw_default), and containers/services got new IDs/names. So that specific hostname may not exist in the new network at that instant.

Let’s lock this down with a repeatable, system-safe checklist.

1) Don’t use curl in PowerShell as the test
On Windows PowerShell, curl is not reliable for this (and you saw it). Instead, use one of these tests:

Test A (best): container-to-container via service name
Compose creates DNS names for services on the network. Use the service name, not the container name.

docker run --rm --network openclaw_default curlimages/curl:latest -i http://openclaw-gateway:18789/
If your service name isn’t openclaw-gateway, check it with:

docker compose ps --format "table {{.Service}}\t{{.Name}}\t{{.State}}\t{{.Ports}}"
It should show openclaw-gateway as the service.

Test B: run curl inside the existing gateway container
docker exec -it openclaw-openclaw-gateway-1 sh -lc "wget -S -O- http://127.0.0.1:18789/ 2>&1 | head -n 15"

3) Why Invoke-WebRequest says “ResponseEnded”
The gateway page is a small HTML shell that immediately drives a WebSocket connection (connect-src … ws: wss: in the CSP you saw). Some Windows HTTP stacks will complain if the connection handling is nonstandard or closes quickly after sending the page. It’s not a reliable health check here.

Use the Docker-network curl (Test A) or the in-container wget (Test B) as your “truth.”

ChatGPT said:
Great data point: inside the gateway container, port 18789 is definitely serving HTTP. So the failure of:

docker run --rm --network openclaw_default curlimages/curl:latest http://openclaw-gateway:18789/
means DNS/networking inside the openclaw_default network is not what we think it is for that one-off container, not that the gateway is down.

This happens on Docker Desktop sometimes because:

your compose project network name is correct, but the docker run --network openclaw_default ... container isn’t actually joining the same network namespace the way we expect (or the network was recreated and the name resolves differently),

or service-name DNS (openclaw-gateway) isn’t available to a container that wasn’t created by compose on that network (rare, but I’ve seen it on desktop setups).

No worries — we can still verify everything deterministically without relying on that DNS.
